          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143129462" href="/book/show/28251241-ceremony">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2361289496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15410345542352237966">This novel is much more than a look at young Native American men shortly after the Second World War and the tension between the traditional/rural and the modern/urban among Native Americans. It is great literature, the best Native American novel I’ve read.<br/><br/>Silko keeps the reader from feeling in any way comfortable in the world she portrays. The past exists with the present, and there is no future to look forward to. It’s a sometimes harrowing novel, in which the reader experiences a good bit of t</span>
  <span id="freeText15410345542352237966" style="display:none">This novel is much more than a look at young Native American men shortly after the Second World War and the tension between the traditional/rural and the modern/urban among Native Americans. It is great literature, the best Native American novel I’ve read.<br /><br />Silko keeps the reader from feeling in any way comfortable in the world she portrays. The past exists with the present, and there is no future to look forward to. It’s a sometimes harrowing novel, in which the reader experiences a good bit of the pain and disorientation the protagonist feels. My only criticism is that the novel would have been better if the third-person narrator had not occasionally left the protagonist to look into other characters. I found the novel’s other imperfections appropriate.</span>

<div id="review_3242641406" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3242641406" />
    <a title="Elizabeth☮ " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/990216-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth☮ " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528771086p2/990216.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<div id="review_1177418547" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1177418547" />
    <a title="Grady" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8034370-grady"><img alt="Grady" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375016989p2/8034370.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1177418547?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Grady" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Grady" href="/user/show/8034370-grady">Grady</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143104919" href="/book/show/52382.Ceremony">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1177418547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13655560638245637577">A part-Native American veteran returns from World War II (including a grueling period as a prisoner of war) and confronts PTSD, American racism, and internalized racial self-loathing. That sounds incredibly downbeat, but two things make this a rewarding, hopeful read. First, Silko's prose is beautiful, direct and vivid, with rich descriptions of light, landscape, and the tactile world. Second, Silko does a couple really powerful things with the structure of her narrative. Since the effect of her</span>
  <span id="freeText13655560638245637577" style="display:none">A part-Native American veteran returns from World War II (including a grueling period as a prisoner of war) and confronts PTSD, American racism, and internalized racial self-loathing. That sounds incredibly downbeat, but two things make this a rewarding, hopeful read. First, Silko's prose is beautiful, direct and vivid, with rich descriptions of light, landscape, and the tactile world. Second, Silko does a couple really powerful things with the structure of her narrative. Since the effect of her techniques is heightened by experiencing them raw, I'm putting my discussion of them behind a spoiler tag. Definitely read the book first.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[In the first half of the novel, while the protagonist Tayo is suffering most intensely from PTSD, the story keeps jumping forward and backwards in time, from the story's present, to Tayo's time in the war, to before the war, to his terrible early childhood. The lurches back and forth are disoriented in a way that echoes Tayo's own dislocation. Once Tayo begins to accept treatment at the hands of an elderly shaman, the narrative structure settles down - and then Silko does something really magical: a major portion of the story happens in a way that only makes sense - retrospectively - within a worldview that sees the spirit and the material world as constantly interconnected. Because it is only evident after the fact that Tayo's world has merged the spirit and the material, a reader who doesn't start with a cultural frame of seeing these worlds as overlapping can be eased into it by the narrative, and only realize after the fact that they've been gifted a double vision. For example, the woman Tayo meets who sleeps with him and teaches him about wild plants is probably Yellow Woman, but that's indicated through her dress and behavior, not through a direct identification. To be clear, Tayo doesn't have a double vision - it's not a problem for him to reconcile what he's experienced; he emerges with a healed, unified vision of the world. But for a secular or materialist reader, what Silko offers is amazing: a chance to feel what it is like to experience realities that cannot be explained or interpreted in material terms, and yet are wholly true. This isn't an abstract exercise; Silko is implicitly arguing that this is how (some) authentic Native American cultures experience(d) reality, and that it offers greater harmony and well-being than the standard Euro-American materialist vision. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> </span>
  <a data-text-id="13655560638245637577" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_14773327" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14773327" />
    <a title="Madeleine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/170060-madeleine"><img alt="Madeleine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479578550p2/170060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14773327?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madeleine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madeleine" href="/user/show/170060-madeleine">Madeleine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6345350-ceremony">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/170060-madeleine?shelf=indigenous-author">indigenous-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14773327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10131794160237889749">This is a famous one so I'm not going to bother with the plot summary thing. Instead I'm just going to rant about how great it is.<br/><br/>Look, <b>there are exceptions to what I am talking about here</b>, but I'm pretty cynical about white American authors writing American Indian/Native American stories. I'm uncomfortable with the occupier telling the occupied's stories. The colonizer gets to show their liberal/progressive credentials by speaking "about" (but very often it's really "for" or "instead of") the c</span>
  <span id="freeText10131794160237889749" style="display:none">This is a famous one so I'm not going to bother with the plot summary thing. Instead I'm just going to rant about how great it is.<br /><br />Look, <b>there are exceptions to what I am talking about here</b>, but I'm pretty cynical about white American authors writing American Indian/Native American stories. I'm uncomfortable with the occupier telling the occupied's stories. The colonizer gets to show their liberal/progressive credentials by speaking "about" (but very often it's really "for" or "instead of") the colonized, and gets to do so in a way that subtly erases the colonization and the ongoing occupation of this land. <br /><br />Sometimes I feel bad about my cynicism, and the fact that when choosing a book about Native American/American Indian issues the author's nationality/race absolutely does matter to me...but then I read things like THIS BOOK, which is completely awesome and which puts colonialism in my white-reader face in a way that, say, Barbara Kingsolver seems incapable of doing. Even though she writes lovely "sympathetic" books about Cherokee and Pueblo characters. Or I spend some time on the Oyate.org website finding other authors to read. And then I think...no, I'll make a point of listening to actual Indigenous voices, thanks...not sympathetic white people like me (who because of the way access to academia and the publishing world work, often get their voices out there much easier). Emphasis on the plural there: voices: Silko's characters speak only for themselves, and in fact she's been criticized by other First Nations writers.<br /><br />I probably sound pissed off and even more like the office leftist than usual...I just really loved this book and was really emotionally affected by it...and I have a bad cold so I'm not expressing myself as well as I (like to think I) usually do. I wasn't even going to "review" this because I knew my review would turn into some sort of weird lengthy rant...but I seem to have gone and done so anyway.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10131794160237889749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16462366163282516618">The superiority of white people -- the notion that this particular race towers over other races -- is merely a construction, so it is possible to deconstruct it. This I learn smoothly from the suffering of Tayo, the main character, a native american, who despite having fought in a war for Uncle Sam side by side with other American citizens, didn't come home a hero but found himself shamefully marginalized just like the rest of his tribe. Through a spiritual journey in a form of ceremonies he pre</span>
  <span id="freeText16462366163282516618" style="display:none">The superiority of white people -- the notion that this particular race towers over other races -- is merely a construction, so it is possible to deconstruct it. This I learn smoothly from the suffering of Tayo, the main character, a native american, who despite having fought in a war for Uncle Sam side by side with other American citizens, didn't come home a hero but found himself shamefully marginalized just like the rest of his tribe. Through a spiritual journey in a form of ceremonies he presented for himself and his ancestors, he managed to regain the pride of himself in particular, and of his people in general. Truly useful for the sometimes inferior me.</span>

<div id="review_3387595756" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3387595756" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202724-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601676054p2/202724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3387595756?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/202724-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6345350-ceremony">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=literaryfiction">literaryfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=nativeamerican">nativeamerican</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3387595756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer632665780518919414">Lyrical writing paints a picture of a life shattered by war, coming home to an entire culture shattered by systemic erasure. Tayo is both Laguna and white, a veteran and a second-class citizen, a member of a family and an orphan. He is untethered, but maybe he always has been. His search for his uncle's missing cattle is a search of something to ground him in the world. It's painful and glorious at the same time. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3387595756">

<div id="review_808337" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/808337" />
    <a title="Andrew Bishop" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62290-andrew-bishop"><img alt="Andrew Bishop" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1177509988p2/62290.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/808337?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew Bishop" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Bishop" href="/user/show/62290-andrew-bishop">Andrew Bishop</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Laguna people, redemption, shamanic healing</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer808337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9447680808205807583">I just read this last year and already I'm going to have to revisit in soon. This book is a manual for post-apocalyptic healing. It couldn't be more necessary in a time when over 20% of the world's species are living ghosts, over 50% are facing extinction, global warming will threaten the (human) population in the hundreds of millions along coastlines and in areas that are increasingly desertifying.<br/><br/>Much less, I would recommend this book to every gringo I know. In my reading, I was presented with</span>
  <span id="freeText9447680808205807583" style="display:none">I just read this last year and already I'm going to have to revisit in soon. This book is a manual for post-apocalyptic healing. It couldn't be more necessary in a time when over 20% of the world's species are living ghosts, over 50% are facing extinction, global warming will threaten the (human) population in the hundreds of millions along coastlines and in areas that are increasingly desertifying.<br /><br />Much less, I would recommend this book to every gringo I know. In my reading, I was presented with a story that I had never known before and would never venture to know were I not willing to step outside the comfort-bounds of my privilege. Noam Chomsky commented about the largest genocide recorded in the world's history, the so-called "New World" conquista, in a DemocracyNow! interview when he said (paraphrasing) "We're only beginning to understand the scale of the atrocities."<br /><br />This is a story of an expansive, magickal and grueling healing process that one man undergoes. It is a story of the shame, pain, faith and humility that he and those in his life experience in this journey. As a metaphor and historical study it is a look at generations of anger and the roots of pain that humans cause one another. Silko flattens stereotypical images of Indians as cartoons of mysticism with unbounded spirituality and environmentalist endowments and the result is ascension toward a redemptive and radical notion of love and power.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9447680808205807583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1274892184?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1274892184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8932801294769045683">This 1980 American Book Award winner pops up on Best Of lists, and it's gotten the Penguin Deluxe Classics treatment, but it's just okay for me. <br/><br/>It's the story of a Native American PTSD-afflicted WWII veteran - that's a lot of adjectives - and it's told in an achronological style that's surprisingly easy to keep track of. Interwoven are excerpts from Laguna myths, along with some original poetry, and those also work way better than you'd think they would. But there was an odd kind of corniness i</span>
  <span id="freeText8932801294769045683" style="display:none">This 1980 American Book Award winner pops up on Best Of lists, and it's gotten the Penguin Deluxe Classics treatment, but it's just okay for me. <br /><br />It's the story of a Native American PTSD-afflicted WWII veteran - that's a lot of adjectives - and it's told in an achronological style that's surprisingly easy to keep track of. Interwoven are excerpts from Laguna myths, along with some original poetry, and those also work way better than you'd think they would. But there was an odd kind of corniness in there that I found distracting. <br /><br />I liked that the love interest, Ts'eh, was <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[imaginary; I thought Silko did a good job of leaving it  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> ambiguous <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ as to whether she was an actual god (or whatever) or just a figment of his tortured imagination. I found a final scene of  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> shocking violence <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ - Emo and Pinkie torture Harley to death out of basically nowhere - jarring and ill-fitting.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Maybe this is just the distance of thirty years, but the central message here - "white people are the worst" - seems cliched to me. I mean, of course we are, everybody knows that. <br /><br />I respect what Silko's up to here: it's a well-written book that achieves what it set out to. But I found myself wanting to check my phone instead of reading it; it failed to really engage me. </span>

<div id="review_3335416107" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3335416107" />
    <a title="Stuart Harden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/109724250-stuart-harden"><img alt="Stuart Harden" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580871616p2/109724250.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3335416107?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart Harden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart Harden" href="/user/show/109724250-stuart-harden">Stuart Harden</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143104919" href="/book/show/52382.Ceremony">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3335416107" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14955524467616146249">We join Tayo's meandering path towards health as he confronts neglect, war trauma, ostracism, and colonial injustice. Silko treats each of these difficult (impossible) subjects with frankness and familiarity, but equally, with compelling nuance. <br/><br/>Silko's sensual and loving descriptions of the mesas, arroyos, hills, people, and plants of Laguna territory animate the story and enthrall the reader. Though I, being a Settler Canadian, cannot fully appreciate Silko's intimate depiction of this particu</span>
  <span id="freeText14955524467616146249" style="display:none">We join Tayo's meandering path towards health as he confronts neglect, war trauma, ostracism, and colonial injustice. Silko treats each of these difficult (impossible) subjects with frankness and familiarity, but equally, with compelling nuance. <br /><br />Silko's sensual and loving descriptions of the mesas, arroyos, hills, people, and plants of Laguna territory animate the story and enthrall the reader. Though I, being a Settler Canadian, cannot fully appreciate Silko's intimate depiction of this particular Pueblo life, her powerful aesthetic and emotional style effectively bridges both time and culture. Ceremony thus achieves one crucial goal of good art and good storytelling: it freights universal experience full with particularity and personality (or perhaps they go the other way around). Ceremony is an important story for any place and any time, though I can only imagine the thrill and comfort this story would bring to someone who calls Laguna home. <br /><br />Silko's Ceremony ends at a beginning: the sunrise which begins a new day and also a new age of Indigenous activism, celebration, and spirituality. We know that our age is one of love and hope in spite of lifetimes of destruction and witchery. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14955524467616146249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_430744136" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/430744136" />
    <a title="Saskia Marijke Niehorster-Cook" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2842575-saskia-marijke-niehorster-cook"><img alt="Saskia Marijke Niehorster-Cook" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314311239p2/2842575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/430744136?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Saskia Marijke Niehorster-Cook" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Saskia Marijke Niehorster-Cook" href="/user/show/2842575-saskia-marijke-niehorster-cook">Saskia Marijke Niehorster-Cook</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143104919" href="/book/show/52382.Ceremony">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2842575-saskia-marijke-niehorster-cook?shelf=native-american">native-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer430744136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11514918163119131061">My son Sasha had this as a read for school book. Half way through it he recommended I read it as well. I think I had already read it for my sociology class in College, but began reading it as well. <br/><br/>It is an assortment of loosely connected and colorful threads of thought that gather vivacity, perspective, and meaning as each strand is woven closer and closer to the others and becomes a flowing tapestry of meaning in this multilayered story of broken hearts, broken lives and broken spirits<br/><br/>On one l</span>
  <span id="freeText11514918163119131061" style="display:none">My son Sasha had this as a read for school book. Half way through it he recommended I read it as well. I think I had already read it for my sociology class in College, but began reading it as well. <br /><br />It is an assortment of loosely connected and colorful threads of thought that gather vivacity, perspective, and meaning as each strand is woven closer and closer to the others and becomes a flowing tapestry of meaning in this multilayered story of broken hearts, broken lives and broken spirits<br /><br />On one level there is a half Mexican, half Pueblo Indian young man who returns from Vietnam in a sea of personal suffering and loss. While there, he loses his best friend and cousin, Rocky. Rocky is the family star, the designated "most likely to succeed" out in the real world of the White Man. Therefore, when he loses Rocky, not only is he losing is best friend and cousin, but he is also seen as the one who should have not come back. He is full of sorrow, regret and guilt.<br /><br />But his loss is greater than that. He has seen a different world than his, a world of chaos, hate and violence too great to leave him mentally intact. Upon his return he suffers a nervous breakdown and he only gets better by medication which renders him emotionally numb, physically ill and spiritually lost.<br /><br />As the story develops we are witness to his childhood in a society where the Native American is seen as a loser, and the half Mexican, half Native American is worthless even to those Native Americans. He is constantly told he is a charity case, and how he shames his family by just being a half breed. Only his uncle Tayo sees something worthy in him, and invests himself emotionally on this child, telling him of his dreams to raise a new sturdy and drought tolerant cattle breed. However, on his return, his uncle is also dead and the cattle dispersed.<br /><br />His friends are other Native American veterans who had a taste of respect and appreciation as men because they were soldiers, but are now devalued as well. The horrors of the war have also taken a toll on their spirits, and they are angry and violent and drink excessively to numb their pain. It is easy for them to blame the white man for their loss of status, the loss of self-respect and going farther back, their loss of land and therefore of a style of living.<br /><br />Among all this loss, all this anger and confusion, come shreds of old Native American songs floating in, sprinkling the story with a second layer. This second layer is a legend of how witchery was practiced and how, even those witches became afraid of a new witch who invented, through a competition to see who would win the most evil ways, white men and invented deep dark evil to visit their land. The other witches awarded the price and begged for the witchery to be stopped, but it could not be stopped...<br /><br />It is a story of how this anti-hero of ours finds inner strength and self perception, and how, through a ceremony, he is healed and brought back to the living. <br /><br />It is, after all, his knowledge of worthlessness, and his understanding that most Native Americans feel likewise that helps him see that there is still hope in returning to the rightful path for him and his people; and that a battle that is still being waged, is not between Native Americans and Whites but between good and evil.<br /><br />While all these is happening, he leaves to find the "lost flock" of cattle and finds it far away in a white man's ranch. While there he has a spiritual encounter with a mountain lion, who inadvertently also saves his life and his cattle. On his way there and back he encounters a woman who cares for him and teaches him about healing plants and love.<br /><br />Meanwhile back at the reservation people are undecided if he is healed or crazy, and in a last struggle to win the war between good and evil, his veteran friends, poisoned by the white man's wars and chaos, turn against him and want to sacrifice him. <br /><br />The story ends with a return to balance, for now, and a re-awakening of the importance of ceremony and of the need to change the ceremony enough to acknowledge the shifts and changes of this ever expanding world of ours.<br /><br />He also comes to realize that this inner knowledge a central part of the outcome of this war.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11514918163119131061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1247740919" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1247740919" />
    <a title="Amber" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3795626-amber"><img alt="Amber" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294852139p2/3795626.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1247740919?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amber" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amber" href="/user/show/3795626-amber">Amber</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143104919" href="/book/show/52382.Ceremony">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3795626-amber?shelf=box-of-shame">box-of-shame</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3795626-amber?shelf=read-for-school">read-for-school</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1247740919" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16541535671461197531">I liked a lot of the themes explored in this book (the power of storytelling, race relations, how war impacts a person, how one finds healing/redemption, etc.), but it was such a fucking <b>slog</b> to get through. Despite discussing the above themes pretty well, the book was boring has hell. Part of the problem was that Tayo never grew on me as a character. I mean, Silko did a really great job describing his PTSD, his fucked up family situation (his mother and passive aggressive aunt, namely), and how</span>
  <span id="freeText16541535671461197531" style="display:none">I liked a lot of the themes explored in this book (the power of storytelling, race relations, how war impacts a person, how one finds healing/redemption, etc.), but it was such a fucking <b>slog</b> to get through. Despite discussing the above themes pretty well, the book was boring has hell. Part of the problem was that Tayo never grew on me as a character. I mean, Silko did a really great job describing his PTSD, his fucked up family situation (his mother and passive aggressive aunt, namely), and how he felt caught between two worlds due to his being only half Native American, but he didn't have much of a personality so it was super hard to care about him personally. I felt bad for him in the way you would feel bad for someone you read about in the newspaper, NOT in the way you would feel bad for a close friend going through hard times. And book characters <b>should</b> feel more like the latter. I should care about them personally rather than in some abstract, distanced way. It just makes it really hard to get involved with their story. Plus, there was A LOT of pointless, repetitive description. Description can be a good way to set a mood, introduce settings/characters/etc., and really immerse you in a story. I love description as a general rule, but this book falls into that WHY ARE WE STILL DESCRIBING THIS ROOM?! category. The description would just go on for far too long and then move on to another just as lengthy description. To the point I would start to zone out. That's the sign you've taken it a bit too far. Lastly, the book was all about Tayo and his journey to find peace with himself and the things he saw/did in the war, but his healing process was very...abstract. He was told he needed to complete the "Ceremony" to find peace, but it is never specified what this Ceremony actually is or how Tayo finally completes it. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[He just suddenly has the revelation that his loved ones are still with him and that he can move on with his life and so on by..wandering in the wilderness? Reclaiming his cattle? I don't know. It wasn't easy to follow his thought process, so it is hard to track how this healing actually happened. Also, he totally leaves his friends to be tortured and dismembered by assholes, and THAT helped his healing process somehow? He is a more complete person because he is okay with leaving friends to die? Sounds more like becoming a sociopath than becoming a whole, healed person. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> The whole process was just kind of baffling and abstract, which also made it hard to care. Or maybe it was explained well, but it was during the times I completely zoned out from boredom. Either way, not marks in this book's favor. This book did do some really smart things, but my not caring is a problem. For me, the whole magic of reading is when books can make me care and have real emotions about fictional characters and fucking words on a page. How cool is that? And I've read plenty of books that make me think AND care, so I know it can be done. So one that fails to make me feel much of anything is kind of missing the point, in my opinion, no matter how smart the themes are. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16541535671461197531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IXVpBiOumQBnJTnnI+E5GxbxVScoE10OzY0bNNh5LnK1W7eLPLp+a9zvqhkIyLmRdKy/F6sAQE2L1t6qnB+KXA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LlIRlB76Nv/65Mak1tow7T+kpkXyPh+D8mluPcHQjWG6fM8ZAe7RlEEuVVr987BnXflMdXEtAsC0MqujhbYpTw==')})
  }

In trouble with the law and in the fight of their lives, the Billy women must find a way, as their ancestors did, to join forces against a devious foe. Humor, toughness, and resourcefulness are the Billys\' only weapons.\n\nUntil the Shell Shaker shows up.\n\nLeAnne Howe, an enrolled member of the Choctaw Nation of Oklahoma, is a fiction writer, playwright, scholar and poet whose writings on Choctaw women are drawn from both personal experience and scholarly research. Her short fiction has appeared in several anthologies, including Through the Eye of the Deer, Returning the Gift, Spider Woman\'s Granddaughters, and Earth Song, Sky Spirit, as well as in journals such as Callaloo and Fiction International.\n\nHowe has read her fiction and lectured throughout the United States, Japan and the Middle East, and her plays have been produced in Los Angeles and New York City. She has also presented programs on recruitment and retention of American Indians at universities and colleges. Currently, she teaches in the English Department at Wake Forest University in Winston-Salem, North Carolina.\n\nIn 1991, Howe received a National Endowment for the Humanities grant to conduct research <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1535932220306583635">Leslie Marmon Silko (born Leslie Marmon; born March 5, 1948) is a Native American writer of the Laguna Pueblo tribe, and one of the key figures in the First Wave of what literary critic Kenneth Lincoln has called the Native American Renaissance.<br/><br/>Silko was a debut recipient of the MacArthur Foundation Grant, now known as the "Genius Grant", in 1981 and the Native Writers' Circle of the Americas Life</span>
  <span id="freeText1535932220306583635" style="display:none">Leslie Marmon Silko (born Leslie Marmon; born March 5, 1948) is a Native American writer of the Laguna Pueblo tribe, and one of the key figures in the First Wave of what literary critic Kenneth Lincoln has called the Native American Renaissance.<br /><br />Silko was a debut recipient of the MacArthur Foundation Grant, now known as the "Genius Grant", in 1981 and the Native Writers' Circle of the Americas Lifetime Achievement Award in 1994. She currently resides in Tucson, Arizona.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia)</span>
